ABSTRACT 
In this thesis, a solution for the heating supply in Raadvad is provided. The current 
heating situation in the town, located north of Copenhagen, is a mix of individual electric 
heat pumps, natural gas boilers and oil boilers. The project aims to evaluate the feasibility 
of different possible scenarios so that local heating can be installed, and heat can be 
produced in a greener way.  
For this purpose, and with an estimation of the heat consumed in all the sinks, 
different cases will be studied to see which is more feasible and could be the best 
alternative to the actual situation. These involve a biomass boiler, a centralized heat pump 
that takes heat from water in the river, individual heat pumps, and a low temperature 
centralized heat pump with boosting heat pumps. 
The results show that the best option is to install a centralized heat pump with a 
capacity of 1.2 MW and with temperatures of the network of 35/70ºC. Thus, future 
developments could involve a more accurate approach to the low temperature network, 
as literature shows high potential for these types of systems in district heating projects. 
 
 
Keywords: district heating, decarbonization, heat pump, heat production, feasibility 
study.

RESUMEN 
En el presente Trabajo de Fin de Máter se propone una solución para el suministro 
de calefacción en Raadvad. La situación actual en la localidad, situada al norte de 
Copenhague, es una mezcla de bombas de calor individuales, calderas de gas natural y 
calderas de gasóleo. El proyecto pretende evaluar la viabilidad de distintos escenarios 
para poder instalar calefacción local, de forma que se genere calor de forma más 
ecológica.  
Para ello, y con una estimación del calor consumido en todos los edificios, se 
estudiarán diferentes casos para ver cuál es óptimo y cuál podría ser la mejor alternativa 
a la situación actual. Estos escenarios son: la utilización de biomasa, una bomba de calor 
centralizada que toma el calor del agua del río, bombas de calor individuales y una bomba 
de calor centralizada de baja temperatura con bombas de calor que aumentan la 
temperatura a la entrada del edificio. 
Los resultados muestran que la mejor opción es instalar una bomba de calor 
centralizada con una capacidad de 1,2 MW y con temperaturas de la red de 35/70ºC. Así 
pues, los desarrollos futuros podrían implicar un planteamiento más preciso de la red de 
baja temperatura, ya que la bibliografía muestra un elevado potencial para este tipo de 
sistemas en proyectos de calefacción urbana. 
 
 
Palabras clave: calefacción urbana, descarbonización, bomba de calor, producción de 
calor, estudio de viabilidad.

1. INTRODUCTION 
1.1 Context 
 
According with the International Energy Agency (IEA), global energy demand is 
set to grow significantly in the following years [1]. This, together with the current political 
situation, makes energy production a key aspect in the actual society. Aligning with the 
Net Zero Scenario [2], it is crucial to adopt alternatives to fossil fuels and to produce heat 
and electricity in a climate-friendly way, thus reducing the climate footprint and 
incorporating more renewable energies. 
Buildings account for about 40% of total energy consumption in the European 
Union [3]. Although progresses are being achieved, direct emissions from heating 
buildings are still increasing, reaching a new high of 2500 Mt of CO2 in 2021. Moreover, 
fossil fuels still meet for 64% of heating energy demand [4]. Thus, the improvement of 
building efficiency has been identified as one crucial point to mitigate these energy and 
environmental issues.  
District heating is an alternative to produce heat that is taking more relevance due 
to its excellent efficiency and cost-effectiveness [5]. Even though globally district heating 
only accounts for 8% of final heat consumption [5], in countries like Denmark have 
significant relevance, as 64% of all private households are connected to district heating 
[6]. 
The share of people connected to DH networks, as well as the total energy 
dedicated to it, is increasing every year as seen in Figure 1, and it is expected that these 
systems will provide 20% of global space heating needs by 2030 [7], as new and stricter 
regulations are being approved. One case is the revision of the Energy Performance of 
Buildings Directive, which has established some MEPS (Minimum Energy Performance 
Standards), that will require the renovation of the 15% worsts buildings in each country 
before 2033 [8]. Therefore, boosting energy efficiency of buildings must be a priority to 
overcome the climate crisis. 
 
==PAGE:12==
        INTRODUCTION 
 
12 
 
 
1.2 Problem statement and motivation 
 
In this context where heat and energy optimization are pursued, the Danish 
Government and the municipalities are taking action in order to improve their heating 
systems. Lyngby-Taarbaek municipality, located in the north of Copenhagen, is so 
concerned about this problem and has developed a strategic energy plan to help out with 
the energy crisis and reduce emissions. This plan focuses primarily on district heating and 
aims, among other aspects, to offer district heating to 98% of households in the 
municipality before 2030 [9]. With this ambitious project, they want to change the current 
situation in the area, where almost 60% of the addresses use natural gas and oil for heating 
[9], and contribute to sustainability. 
This project focuses on Raadvad, a small village near Dyrehavn in Lyngby-
Taarbaek municipality. It was one of the first Danish industries, dating from the 17th 
century, which has been renovated in the past years and nowadays is composed of a 
Figure 1.- Evolution of the share of energy use in different countries (Pomianowski et al. 2020) 
==PAGE:13==
       Feasibility study of a local heating system in Raadvad 
 
 
 
13 
housing association and a group of small manufacturing industries unified under the name 
of Raadvad Fabrik.  
The actual heating situation in Raadvad is a mix of individual heat pumps, natural 
gas boilers and electrics boilers. Following the strategic energy plan developed by 
Lyngby-Taarbaek municipality, a local solution for district heating is needed in Raadvad, 
as it is too far from the district heating network to be connected. Due to the history and 
age of the buildings, the solution to develop must be respectful with the environment and 
the buildings, as many conservation regulations are applied. 
 
 
1.3 Objectives 
 
This project aims to evaluate the feasibility of different possible solutions so that 
local heating can be installed in the town of Raadvad, and heat can be produced in a 
greener way. For this purpose, firstly, the total heating demand will be calculated with 
the data of each building from BBR (Building and Housing Register). After this, using 
EnergyPRO, different cases will be studied to see which is more feasible and could be the 
best alternative to this specific project. With the results obtained from these scenarios, a 
feasibility study will be done to reach the better solution regarding the technical feasibility 
and conservation of the area, and to investigate which heating system solution could fit 
better. 
In line with the above explained, the research questions of this study are the ones 
as follows: 
 What are the best ways to decarbonize heating, and how district heating 
can play a key role in this process. 
 How temperature changes in the network affect to the heat losses, and how 
this could be beneficial to future district heating systems. 
==PAGE:14==
        INTRODUCTION 
 
14 
Moreover, the project will also contribute to the following Sustainable 
Development Goals (SDGs), which are listed in [10]: 
- 
7: Affordable and clean energy 
- 
11: Sustainable cities and communities 
 
 
1.4 Thesis overview 
 
The present thesis is organized as follows: 
- 
Section 1 provides an overview of the current energy issues, focusing on the 
need of decarbonization. It also introduces the problem to be analysed in this 
study. 
- 
Section 2 presents a comprehensive review of relevant literature, covering key 
concepts that will be utilized in the project. It explores topics such as the 
district heating technology, heat pumps, and various energy sources that can 
be used. 
- 
Section 3 describes the methodology employed throughout the thesis, firstly 
for obtaining the heating demand, and then for developing the cases that are 
going to be studied. 
- 
Section 4 presents and analyses the results obtained from the previous section, 
comparing the different scenarios to identify the optimal solution.

1.5 AI use in the thesis 
 
Today, AI is playing an essential role in our lives. Not only it enables companies to 
work efficiently but it also can be utilized in creative projects. An example of this is Coca-
Cola’s last advertisement, which was entirely created using AI technology [11]. 
In addition, as is well known, OpenAI has developed ChatGPT (Chatbot Generative 
Pre-trained Transformer), an advanced AI software specifically designed to simulate 
conversations with humans. Recent literature highlights the crucial role this powerful tool 
is playing in scientific, as enables faster and easier writing [12]. 
Regarding future advancements, there is a growing focus on developing visual 
elements such as tables and figures within AI systems. These elements aim to assist in 
summarizing complex data, thus offering users a clearer understanding of the information 
[13]. 
In this thesis, AI was mainly used to enhance the readability of the text, summarize 
data, and as a tool of providing suggestions on how explain some complex concepts. This 
improves the coherence and the quality of the writing. 
However, it is important to address the question of whether this software can fully 
replace human work. It is crucial to emphasize that in the process of scientific article 
writing, the expertise, judgment, and responsibility of human researchers are 
indispensable. While AI technology can enhance productivity and efficiency, it cannot 
replace the critical thinking and supervision provided by human researchers. The human 
element remains essential in ensuring the quality and reliability of scientific work.

2. LITERATURE REVIEW 
The aim of this chapter is to synthesize and explain the main concepts to be used 
in the project for a proper comprehension of it. Furthermore, it will be completed with 
data and findings from various papers and investigations. 
 
2.1 District heating 
 
District Heating (DH) is a heating system that delivers heat to multiple buildings 
and industries from a centralized energy source. The heat is transported and distributed 
among all the households through an underground network of pipes, using water-based 
systems with heat exchangers in order to supply space heating and domestic hot water. 
The main goal for the utilization of DH systems is to promote heat with the most benefits 
to society while reducing the energy supply’s dependency on fossil fuels. 
DH technology is primarily established in high density areas where there is a high 
demand for heat such as cities or urban areas. However, it can also be installed in smaller 
areas that are connected to the main DH network, or even as local DH projects which are 
disconnected from the network. In Figure 2, it can be seen a generic DH system. This is 
composed of a heat source, that can be derived from various origins; a transmission 
network, which works at high temperatures and has a high capacity; and a distribution 
system, which operates at lower temperatures due to smaller pipe dimensions. 
Additionally, the system has exchangers between the different networks and the end-
users, as closed loops are employed. Depending on the project requirements, one or more 
storage tanks may also be incorporated into the system. 
In Denmark, the production of heat for DH systems is mainly from combined 
heating and power (CHP) plants, which account for more than 60% of the total heat 
produced [14]. CHP plants generate both heat and electricity from the same energy 
source, making them highly efficient. Other technologies that are utilized in these systems 
are gas boilers, biomass boilers or heat pumps. 
==PAGE:18==
        LITERATURE REVIEW 
 
18 
 
The figure below illustrates the changes in DH production in Denmark over the 
past few years. It can be observed that in general terms DH production has increased over 
time, as well as the number of DH units (heat boilers). This rise in DH production is 
attributable to the lower prices of electricity, as reported by DEA [14]. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
This evolution through the years in DH networks means that four different 
generations can be identified based on their characteristics and technology. The trend over 
these generations has been to lower the temperature in order to decrease losses and 
increase efficiency. 
Figure 3.- DH production (DEA 2015) 
Figure 2.- Illustration of DH system 
==PAGE:19==
       Feasibility study of a local heating system in Raadvad 
 
 
 
19 
The first generation was mainly introduced in the USA in the 1880s and used 
steam as a heat carrier. Although these types of networks are still present in some cities, 
the big majority have been replaced by newer technologies due to their high heat losses 
and the risk of severe explosions of steam [15]. 
In the 1930s the heat carrier was changed from steam to pressurized water, 
reaching supply temperatures well above 100ºC. These systems reached higher values of 
efficiency than the first generation, but still suffered a big amount of heat losses. With the 
introduction of certain policies, it was possible to accomplish a suitable expansion in 
urban areas [15]. 
With the third generation, supply temperatures below 100ºC were achieved. This 
generation appeared in the 1970s mainly in the Scandinavian countries, and oil was 
beginning to be replaced with coal, biomass and waste [15]. 
The increased interest in energy efficiency and in renewable energy brought the 
fourth generation of district heating, which is designed in order to achieve 
decarbonization. In [15] it is mentioned the challenges that DH has to face in these years, 
such as low supply temperatures to reduce losses, and the ability to be integrated into 
smart energy systems including district cooling. A wide variety of projects using low-
temperature systems have already been implemented successfully in many countries, as 
stated in [16]. 
Recently, a new generation of DH is starting to appear. Lund et al. [17] mention 
the fifth generation of district heating (5GDH), which reduces even more transmission 
losses by using ultra-low temperatures that requires local or individual boosting in order 
to reach the temperature levels required for its use. However, these authors consider 
5GDH different from the previous four as heat pumps at each consumer should be 
installed to adjust the supply temperature to the specific requirements, thus allowing a 
parallel development between 4th and 5th generations. 
The main principles of 5GDH are explained in [13], where it is emphasized that 
the system is designed to reduce waste and increase efficiency by implementing a closed 
loop that re-uses the return flow. In addition, the system prioritizes the use of local heat 
sources to maximize the benefits for the local area of the network, and is demand-driven, 
meaning that heat is only produced when it is necessary. 
==PAGE:20==
        LITERATURE REVIEW 
 
20 
Denmark has a long history of utilizing DH as a primary heating solution. The 
first CHP plant was built in 1903, and this was quickly followed by the development of 
DH networks. By the 1970s, around 30% of homes in Denmark were using these networks 
for heating.  
 In 1979 the first heating supply was approved, which established the framework 
for planning and implementing DH projects in Denmark. The law stipulated that 
municipalities were responsible for planning the heating demand and proposing solutions 
for future heating supply. Today, this responsibility still rests with municipalities, as they 
are responsible for updating and approving heating projects. 
Since then, multiple actions have been taken in order to promote and develop DH, 
like the ban on electric heating in new buildings in 1988, or the taxes applied to fuels in 
the 80s to promote environmentally friendly energy. As a result of all these measures, 
Denmark nowadays provides DH to almost 70% of households, with a total supply of 122 
PJ [14]. 
 
 
2.2 Legislation 
 
DH projects must be in accordance with the Heat Supply Act, which emphasizes 
the importance of “promoting the most economical, including environmentally friendly, 
use of energy for the heating of buildings and the supply of hot water and reducing the 
energy supply's dependence on fossil fuels” [18]. This means that the project must 
demonstrate that provides a positive impact on both the company and the end-users. Thus, 
the municipality must ensure that the heating planning is coordinated with other relevant 
legislation including environmental requirements. 
In addition, if the project is conducted in a Natura 2000 area, as is the case for 
Raadvad project [19] where the Mill river is designated as so, an impact assessment must 
be conducted, taking into account the conservation objective for the area in question, as 
specified in the ‘Order on the designation and administration of international nature 
conservation areas and the protection of certain species [20]. This assessment could study, 
==PAGE:21==
       Feasibility study of a local heating system in Raadvad 
 
 
 
21 
in the project carried out, how the Mill River will be affected in relation to water 
temperature or possible leakage from the plant. 
Furthermore, the watercourse must be in concordance with the Nature 
Conservation Act [21], and no changes may be made in lakes with an area larger than 100 
m2, thus having to carry out an initial or consequence assessment. 
Different permissions and approvals will be required in projects involving heat 
extraction from different sources. The specific permissions that are necessary can vary 
depending on factors such as the heat source and the chosen technology. 
 
 
2.3 Heat pumps 
 
Each District Heating system design is unique to a particular project and must 
conform to its specific characteristics in order to maximize its benefits. Olsthoorn et al. 
[22] defined three key factors that influence the heat supply: available energy source, 
topography of suppliers/end-users, and DHW needs. Among these, the availability of 
energy sources is crucial to the installation of a DH system, as inadequate conditions 
could make the project unfeasible. 
Various energy sources can play a role in a DH system, from coal or natural gas 
to waste heat and renewable technologies. Typically, heat from different sources like 
water, air, the soil or the sun is extracted and used, keeping the conventional ways of 
heating as backup systems to support the network.  
Heat pumps are one of the most used technologies in district heating, as they can 
be used for various applications and can take advantage of different heat sources. They 
enable extracting heat from low-temperature sources and transforming it into high-
temperature heat by adding work. This capacity of extracting heat from one source makes 
these devices not only capable of producing heat, but also they can act as cooling 
elements. 
==PAGE:22==
        LITERATURE REVIEW 
 
22 
 Although in essence they are not a renewable technology, they are considered as 
such as they have a coefficient of performance (COP) higher than 1, and because the 
electricity used can come from a renewable source like the wind. COP is a measure that 
represents the ratio of the amount of heat generated to the electricity consumed, as seen 
in the following equation: 
𝐶𝑂𝑃= 𝑄̇
𝑊̇ =
𝑇ℎ
𝑇ℎ−𝑇𝑐
 
Where 𝑄̇ is the total amount of heat produced, 𝑊̇  represents the electricity 
consumed, 𝑇ℎ is the temperature of the heat source, and 𝑇𝑐 is the temperature of the cold 
source. As seen in equation (1), COP in heat pumps is directly related to temperature 
level, and usually varies between 3 and 4, which means that the amount of heat produced 
triples the amount of power needed. 
Heat pumps can be classified into two different types: mechanical heat pumps and 
thermal (absorption) heat pumps. 
Mechanical heat pumps, which are the most widespread type, are electrically 
driven, and their working principle is through a vapor-compression cycle. These are 
integrated of an evaporator, compressor, condenser, and expansion valve, with a 
circulating refrigerant that evaporates at low temperatures. 
The closed cycle starts with the refrigerant absorbing the heat from the source in 
the evaporator. Hence the compressor increases the pressure of the refrigerant, changing 
the state into saturated vapor. In the condenser, the refrigerant yields the heat to the low-
temperature sink, and finally, the expansion valve reduces the pressure to its initial value. 
The basic configuration of the cycle and its P-h diagram can be seen in Figure 4 
and Figure 5 respectively. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:23==
       Feasibility study of a local heating system in Raadvad 
 
 
 
23 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
The working fluid selected for the vapor-compression cycle allows the heat 
pump's performance to be tailored to the characteristics of the source and to the network 
of which is part. The choice of working fluid influences various factors such as the size 
of the compressor, the materials that can be utilized, and the required safety measures, 
Figure 5.- P-h diagram of a vapor-compression cycle 
Figure 4.- Simple configuration of a vapor-compression cycle 
==PAGE:24==
        LITERATURE REVIEW 
 
24 
among others. In addition, these fluids must achieve some characteristics, such as they 
must be safe, avoiding corrosive, toxic and flammable fluids; and they must suit the 
working conditions of the system. In Denmark, HFC refrigerants are not allowed when 
the heat pump's charge exceeds 10 kg, due to their high Global Warming Potential (GWP) 
and low Ozone Depletion Potential. In such cases, natural refrigerants such as CO2, 
ammonia and propane must be employed, as specified in [23]–[25]. 
On the other side thermal heat pumps are heat-driven, and they use steam for 
operation. Its advantage is that they can use waste heat from the process of heating in a 
DH system, so they can work with no real heat consumption. However, absorption heat 
pumps usually have a constant COP of around 1.7, which is not dependent on temperature 
level. This value is significantly lower than mechanical HP which has COP with values 
at around 3-5 [25]. This type of HP is based on the absorption cycle, and a mixture of 
water and ammonia is typically used in this case as the working fluid. This fluid is a 
liquid-vapor mix, where the gas is almost pure refrigerant (ammonia), and the liquid 
solution is poor in refrigerant. 
Another important aspect of heat pump projects is their regulation and legislation. 
This framework has changed in the last years, which can contribute positively to the 
economy of a project. The PSO (Public Service Obligation) tariff, which was used to 
cover costs for renewables, has been completely removed in 2022 [26]. Moreover, the 
electric heating tax has been reduced by a total of 25 øre/kWh from 2017 to 2021, and a 
support scheme of up to 15% of establishment costs has been stated [25]. This shows the 
importance of heat pumps in future energy systems, and the desire to develop these types 
of projects. 
 
 
2.4 Heat sources 
 
As stated above, the chosen energy type varies depending on the characteristics of 
the project and its locally available resources. In [25] it is highlighted that apart from 
==PAGE:25==
       Feasibility study of a local heating system in Raadvad 
 
 
 
25 
identifying the sources, it is equally important to investigate their potential: temperature, 
availability, variation over the year… 
There is a wide range of technologies and sources that can be used in DH projects, 
some of them are briefly reviewed in this section.  
- 
Geothermal and groundwater sources play a significant role in providing 
renewable and sustainable heat. Projects using these sources take advantage 
of hot water available underground, thus heat is extracted through boreholes 
or drainage fields. It is important in this process the use of exchangers to 
transfer the heat effectively. To further increase the temperature, absorption 
heat pumps are commonly employed [27]. Groundwater reservoirs not only 
serve as heat sources with heat pump solutions but also function as storage 
systems, thus being used for cooling in summer and heating in winter. Usually, 
water is pumped back to the ground, but it should be done at a different 
location from the extraction site. Utilizing groundwater as a heat source offers 
several advantages. The temperature remains relatively constant throughout 
the year, typically around 8ºC, providing a reliable and consistent source of 
heat. In addition, boreholes, with regular maintenance, can have a long 
lifespan of up to 100 years. Another system that takes advantage of ground 
heat is Termonet [28], which consists of a network of uninsulated ground 
pipes. The network supplies multiple heat pumps and aims to create a balance 
between heating and cooling demand, as shown in Figure . 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Figure 6.- Termonet network (Termonet Danmark) 
==PAGE:26==
        LITERATURE REVIEW 
 
26 
 
- 
Biomass plants produce both heat and electricity by using steam at high 
temperatures and pressure. These boilers have also high efficiencies, reaching 
up to 94% in some cases [29]. Different fuels can be used in biomass plants, 
such as wood pellets and wood chips. Wood pellets are the simplest ones, as 
they don’t need any special pre-treatment. However, they also have a higher 
price compared to wood chips, according to [27]. Biomass can also be utilized 
in smaller forms, like biomass boilers. In [22] it is emphasized that in Europe 
is common to couple biomass boilers with solar thermal systems. Although 
greenhouse emissions are produced in a biomass boiler, this source is 
considered as renewable one by many authors due to the cyclical nature of 
biomass as a fuel. 
- 
Waste is a commonly used source of energy in DH projects. According to [30], 
it is considered a renewable source of energy, as it contributes to emissions 
reduction and helps to enhance the quality of the environment. However, as 
Oolsthoorn et al. highlight in [22], some European countries still have 
concerns due to the emissions of harmful gases. The highest potential for 
utilizing waste heat is achieved when low DH temperatures are utilized, 
reaching heat efficiencies of 70-80%. Various authors also remark that 
although the amount of waste is fairly constant throughout the year, using local 
waste heat is a complex type of DH network as the composition of waste is 
always changing, while emissions must be kept as constant as possible. 
- 
Solar energy in DH systems has been gaining importance in the past years due 
to the expansion of renewable energy. The problem with this source is the 
mismatch between the solar irradiance and the peak heating demand, so it is 
key the presence of a thermal energy storage (TES) to fulfill heat demand. As 
efficiency and performance decreases with an increase in the temperature, 
solar systems are going to play a significant role in the new generations of DH 
[27]. 
 
==PAGE:27==
       Feasibility study of a local heating system in Raadvad 
 
 
 
27 
 
2.5 Temperatures 
 
As seen in subsection 2.1 “District heating”, the evolution in DH systems has 
come together with the increase in efficiency and the consequent reduction in the 
temperature used. The current trend is to continue lowering the temperatures of the district 
heating network as low as possible, in order to reduce heat losses in transmission and 
increase efficiencies, thus enabling the integration of new waste sources. 
In terms of heat sources and the technologies used for taking advantage of them, 
using low distribution temperatures (both supply and return) has multiple benefits as it 
has been widely seen in literature [15], [17], [31], [32]: higher power-to-heat ratios in 
steam CHP plants, higher heat recovery from flue gas condensation, cost-efficient 
integration of renewable energy technologies and economically feasible integration of 
even more waste heat sources. The COP of heat pumps is also affected by the network 
temperature, with performance decreasing as the supply temperature increases, reaching 
low values of performance when the supply in the transmission network is 90ºC or above, 
in accordance with [33]. 
In the actual context, according to Oomen et al. [34] the forward temperature 
varies from 70ºC to 120ºC, and the return goes from 35ºC to 55ºC. This study shows that 
with low network temperatures, consumer costs and emissions decrease by 2.8% and 
6.4% respectively, and that the integration of large heat pumps can be very beneficial for 
these types of networks, also achieving reductions in costs and emissions. The authors 
recommend a supply temperature of 65ºC-70ºC, as lower temperatures would require 
higher investments. Elmeggard et al. conclude in [24] that for conventional sources, high-
temperature networks are better than low-temperature solutions, as the decrease in heat 
loss does not compensate the electricity demand to cover the energy consumption. 
However, assume that DH will tend to go to lower temperatures. 
Otherwise, in Lund et al. [15] further reduction in temperatures is proposed, 
reaching a value of 40ºC which is the minimum that actual space heating technology 
allows. 
==PAGE:28==
        LITERATURE REVIEW 
 
28 
As losses occur mainly in the transmission pipes, 4th and 5th GDH with ultra-low 
temperatures are increasingly being utilized to minimize them. While lower temperatures 
have several advantages as seen above, it is also important to consider other factors. 
Low temperatures are usually sufficient to cover the space heating demand, but it 
may not be enough for domestic hot water (DHW), thus temperature boosting is needed. 
Therefore, decentralized heat pumps are used to reach the required temperature for 
consumers. With regard to this, Østergaard and Andersen [35] concluded that using 
booster heat pumps alongside a central heat pump can help to reduce grid losses and gas 
consumption. Systems with boosting heat pumps provide the opportunity to optimize the 
temperatures of the network and reduce costs, whether it is for heating or for cooling 
purposes. 
However, implementing ultra-low temperature systems requires consideration of 
the potential risk of legionella, a bacteria that grows between temperatures of 35 and 37ºC 
and can seriously affect people's health. Many countries have directives to prevent it, 
stipulating that hot water for domestic use must be heated up to 60ºC due to sanitary 
reasons [36]. In Denmark, there are also legislations to prevent the growth of 
microorganisms including legionella, so that DHW has to be kept above 50ºC in normal 
operation, and at a minimum of 45ºC during peak demands [37]. Temperature boosting 
and avoiding DHW storage can help to address this issue. Although thermal treatment of 
water is the most common way of preventing Legionella, water treatment with UV light 
or an installation of a disinfection system can also be an option to reduce the risk, as it is 
suggested by Schmidt et al. [16]. 
 
 
2.6 Heat losses 
 
Heat losses in district heating systems play a significant role in determining the 
overall energy efficiency and operational costs of them. Typically, heat losses are about 
20%, according to various sources [35], [38]. These losses vary from case to case due to 
several parameters within the system, such as length, pipe dimension, location, insulation 
==PAGE:29==
       Feasibility study of a local heating system in Raadvad 
 
 
 
29 
level of pipework, and the amount of hot water usage. Although heat losses can occur in 
different elements of the network like heat exchangers or heat pumps, most of them take 
place in the distribution network, as heat and pressure losses. Moreover, if the network 
has a circulation loop to keep the flow in the network constant, studies show that losses 
in this part can account for 73% of the total, as stated by [31]. This highlights the 
importance of optimizing distribution and circulation pipes to minimize energy losses. 
Heat losses are higher in decentralized district heating systems, due to low 
connection density. To address this issue, many DH utilities have focused on reducing 
the operating temperatures, thus reducing the temperature gradient between the DH fluid 
and the external environment. This is a potential solution to mitigate heat losses, 
according to Vivian et al. [39]. By optimizing temperature settings, significant 
improvements in energy efficiency and reduction of heat losses can be achieved. Research 
suggests that by transitioning from higher operating temperatures, such as 95/55°C, to 
lower temperatures like 60/40°C, significant heat losses can be saved. In [27] it is 
indicated that this transition can potentially reduce heat losses by approximately 35%. 
Sorknæs et al. [40] indicates also that the reduction of losses in low-temperature networks 
of less than 60ºC is always close to 40%, independently of the dimension of the pipes. 
The dimension of the pipe also affects heat losses within a network. Reducing the 
heat transmission area of the pipe, which can be achieved by decreasing the diameter, 
helps minimize heat losses. 
Other factors that affect the amount of heat losses, although in a lesser degree, in 
a DH network are the heat transmission factor from water to pipe, the pipe wall material 
and the heat transmission from pipe to soil, according to [41]. 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:30==
        LITERATURE REVIEW 
 
30 
 
 
 
==PAGE:31==
       Feasibility study of a local heating system in Raadvad 
 
 
 
31 
3. METHODOLOGY AND DATA COLLECTION 
In this chapter, the methodology used throughout the project is presented, which 
is divided into two main parts. Figure 7 illustrates the framework of the procedure 
undertaken. 
 
The first section, 3.1 “Heating demand”, explains the methodology for 
determining the heat demand and other relevant parameters for conducting the 
simulations in the software utilized. The second part, which comprises sections 3.2 “Base 
case. CO2 emissions”, 3.3 “Simulation”, and 3.4 “Scenarios”, focuses on the techno-
economical optimization and analysis of the different scenarios assessed. In essence, the 
adopted methodology highlights the significance of simulating various scenarios using 
the appropriate input data to ensure a thorough feasibility study. 
 
 
 
Figure 7.- Procedure for estimating the heating demand and mass flowrate 
==PAGE:32==
        METHODOLOGY AND DATA COLLECTION 
 
32 
 
3.1 Heating demand 
 
The goal of this initial section is to estimate the heat demand for each house 
number in Raadvad to ensure that the DH system solution implemented has the 
appropriate size. To accomplish this, a combination of two types of data will be utilized: 
building data and heat load profiles. 
3.1.1 Building data 
Building data in Denmark is overseen and controlled by the public administration 
and can be accessed through BBR (Bygnings- og Boligregistret). The Building and 
Housing register contains information on buildings, residential and commercial units 
from all the houses in the country, as well as information on annual energy consumption 
[42].  Among all the data available in the register, the main information used for this 
project is: 
- 
Address (street and number) 
- 
Area (footprint area and total area) 
- 
Year of construction and reconstruction 
- 
Building code 
This data is overseen by the municipality but is the owner of the property who is 
responsible for keeping the information updated and informing the municipality of any 
changes made. Therefore, there may be instances where the data does not reflect the actual 
conditions of the house/building, and some house numbers may not be listed on the 
register. To address this issue, some assumptions have been made, estimating the data for 
properties not included in BBR based on the information from neighboring buildings. For 
example, house numbers 14, 16, 18 and 20 do not appear on the register, but they are 
considered with the same characteristics as number 12 as they all are semi-detached 
houses in the same building. Furthermore, data from the report made by a consultancy 
[43] was also utilized to complete the information from all the buildings. This is the case 
of sink 9, which is not similar to any buildings around. In this case, the area of the house 
==PAGE:33==
       Feasibility study of a local heating system in Raadvad 
 
 
 
33 
has been determined using the information provided in the report, while the year of 
construction and building used is assumed to be equal to sink 7. 
Moreover, there are a few buildings (a restaurant and an industrial building) that 
are located outside of Lyngby-Taarbaek municipality, specifically in Rudersdal 
kommune. Consequently, the BBR data for these buildings do not appear in the register. 
As a result, these buildings have been excluded from the study as it has been determined 
that this data is not essential for the completion of the project. 
3.1.2 Heat load profiles 
Additionally, heat load profiles have been utilized to make an estimation of the 
heat demand of each property. These profiles represent the hourly variation of heat 
demand per unit area (kWh/m2) throughout a year for a specific house type, and are very 
useful to understand the occupant’s behavior in relation to heat consumption. These 
profiles have been created using degree-days for each hour of the year utilizing the data 
from buildings in Lyngby. Therefore, two parameters are required as inputs to calculate 
the heat consumption for residential buildings: year of construction or reconstruction and 
type of house. 
These data can be obtained from BBR so that a load profile can be assigned to 
each residential building by using the tables below:

Once each house number has a load profile assigned, the heating demand can be 
calculated both by using the hourly demand and the square meters with the following 
equation: 
𝐷= ∑𝑑∙𝑎𝑟𝑒𝑎

Where: 
- 
D is the heating demand of a year for a house number in kWh. 
- 
d represents the load profile for each hour of the year in kWh/m2. 
- 
area is the total area of the building in m2. 
To calculate the heating demand in kW, the results obtained can be divided by 
8760 hours/year. 
Finally, once the heating demand for each number and for the whole system is 
estimated, it is also worth calculating the mass flowrate, to be able to correctly size the 
system and the technology used. The following equation is used for obtaining this 
parameter: 
𝑚̇ =
𝑄
𝐶𝑃∙∆𝑇 
Where: 
- 
𝑚̇  is the mass flowrate in kg/s. 
- 
𝑄 is the heating demand in kW. 
- 
𝐶𝑃 is the specific heat of water, with a value of 4.19 kJ/Kg∙𝐾 
- 
∆𝑇 is the temperature difference between the entrance and the exit of the 
system, which is called the target and supply temperature. 
This equation is applied to the hourly heat demand for each profile, selecting the 
peak value of the year to prevent overloading the system. As the mass flowrate is needed 
in kg/h, the results for each sink in the system are obtained by multiplying by 3600 the 
values obtained. Finally, the sum of all the mass flowrates is then used to dimension the 
heat source and the technology used, in order to adapt and dimension the system to meet 
the requirements of heat. The total mass flowrate will be over-dimensioned a 20%, as 
stated in 2.6 ‘Heat losses’. 
All the calculations for estimating the heat demand and the mass flowrate can be 
found in A “Heat demand”.

3.2 Base case. CO2 emissions 
 
The base case, which reflects the actual heating situation in Raadvad, will serve 
as a reference for comparing with the proposed scenarios presented in section 3.4 
”Scenarios”, and will help to assess the environmental impact of the current situation. 
The comparison will be made in terms of CO2 emissions, so for each technology, 
the emission factor should be applied. Although emission factor data can vary depending 
on the source [44]–[46], the IPCC's emission factors in kgCO2/kWh [47] will be 
employed in this project, as they are widely used.  
It should be noted, however, that IPCC does not include a value for the emission 
factor of electricity. This value can vary depending on the specific method of electricity 
generation, as there are no direct emissions. For electricity, the emissions factor varies 
more than for other energy sources, but the one utilized in the study will be the value 
provided by the Danish Energy Agency [48]. 
The values for the emission factor are shown in the table below.

The existing heating situation in Raadvad is comprised of various individual 
technologies, such as heat pumps, oil boilers, electric heating and wood/pellet stoves, as 
indicated in the report prepared by a consultancy [43]. Additional information was 
gathered through meetings with stakeholders involved on the project. 
The heating sources among the 36 sinks stablished for the realization of the project 
are distributed as follows: 
Table 5.- Distribution of heating technologies in Raadvad

It should be noted that there are some sinks that use more than one heating 
technology, so that is the reason why the number of sinks in the table does not match the 
total number of sinks. This situation is particularly common with wood stove technology, 
which often serves as a complement to natural gas or electricity systems. In these cases 
where multiple technologies are used, emissions are calculated based on the proportion 
of heating provided by each technology. This is the case of sink 9, where 68% of the 
heating demand is covered by a natural gas boiler, while the remaining 32% is met using 
electricity. In instances where this data is not available in the report, a 50-50 distribution 
of heating is assumed, as done in sink 17. 
In addition, there are certain sinks for which no data is available in both the BBR 
register and the report. As done in previous sections, in such cases the heating technology 
will be assumed to be the same as the neighboring sinks. However, this assumption cannot 
be made for sinks 1, 2, and 13. As a result, these sinks will not be taken into account in 
the initial emission calculations. 
As outlined in the report, sinks from number 4 to 10 share a common heating 
supply derived from a natural gas boiler, but these buildings also expressed their interest 
on changing to DH. Therefore, this installation along with the natural gas boilers that 
power the industries’ association, can serve as a backup system, capable of providing heat 
==PAGE:38==
        METHODOLOGY AND DATA COLLECTION 
 
38 
in case of failure of the district heating network. This redundancy ensures a reliable heat 
supply and minimizes potential disruptions to the heating system. 
 
 
3.3 Simulation 
 
This section covers an introduction of EMB3Rs platform and EnergyPRO, which 
are appropriate software for simulating potential scenarios. The primary objective is to 
utilize these platforms to assess the feasibility of the different scenarios in generating heat 
in a greener way. 
o Simulation in EMB3Rs platform 
EMB3Rs in an EU-funded platform that enables industries and other heat sources 
to utilize their excess heat [49]. This tool can help to optimize the use of renewable 
energies and improve energy performance. The platform is able to assess the feasibility 
of new heating projects, thus identifying the best technical solutions and reducing CO2 
emissions. In this project, the platform will be used to analyze different solutions for a 
DH project, leveraging natural sources as heat sources instead of waste heat. 
EMB3Rs platform is composed of five different modules in which information is 
transferred from one to another, as seen in Figure 8. This allows doing a complete 
simulation to investigate the technical and economic potential of the heating system. 
Figure 8.- Modules of the platform inspired by EMB3Rs 
==PAGE:39==
       Feasibility study of a local heating system in Raadvad 
 
 
 
39 
 CF Module: The Core Functionalities Module allows the full characterization 
of the sinks and sources created, thus providing technical information to the 
following modules. It also performs a first analysis of the heat supply and 
demand. 
 GIS Module: The Geographical Information System Module provides a 
possible network solution, including losses and investment costs. This is done 
through the existing Open Street Map (OSM) Road Network, which connects 
all the sources and sinks. 
 TEO Module: The Techno-Economic Optimization Module identifies the 
most suitable technologies to meet the heating demand and match between 
sources and sinks. This module considers several technologies for each source 
and sink based on the temperature level of the source. 
 Market Module: This, outputs different economic indicators, with the analysis 
of short-term and long-term markets and the simulation of various market 
structures. 
 Business Model Module: This last module provides several financial values 
like Net Present Value (NPV), Internal Rate of Return (IRR), Payback Period 
(PP) and Levelized Cost of Heat (LCOH), taking as inputs values such as 
CAPEX or OPEX. 
For the completion of the simulations in the platform, the inputs needed are the 
mass the mass flowrate, the yearly heating consumption and the daily period in which 
heating is needed for each sink, but also it is necessary to include the fluid Cp (which is 
4.19 KJ/Kg·K as the fluid used is water), and the supply and target temperature of the 
distribution network. The supply temperature is the temperature of the fluid on the sink 
side, and the target temperature is the one that should be achieved. 
For the source, the same values are needed. But as specified in previous sections, 
the mass flowrate will be the sum of all mass flowrates from the sinks, oversized a 20% 
to compensate losses. 
 
==PAGE:40==
        METHODOLOGY AND DATA COLLECTION 
 
40 
o Simulation in EnergyPRO Software 
EnergyPRO provides users with a comprehensive overview of energy 
projects. This software is not only suitable for heat pumps, but it can also be applied to 
cogeneration plants, hybrid energy systems, tri-generation plants, and biomass plants 
[50]. Additionally, as a leading software, it is applicable to photovoltaic and wind energy 
projects as well. The main objective of this project is to simulate four scenarios involving 
different heat pump designs and biomass utilization. 
Upon completing the simulation, EnergyPRO generates technical and 
economic reports that facilitate a thorough understanding of the dynamics within a 
complex energy system. According to the objectives of this project, these reports enable 
users to draw conclusions and optimize the design of each specific scenario. 
The system is based on a modular structure which makes it possible to 
customize the many features to suit the needs of the user, as shown in Figure 9. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
The Design module plays a crucial role in energy plants as it involves the 
calculation and optimization of production and operational economics. As shown in the 
figure above, this module occupies a central position in the structure. The interaction 
among the other modules is dependent on this module. 
Figure 9.- Modules of the software inspired by EnergyPRO 
==PAGE:41==
       Feasibility study of a local heating system in Raadvad 
 
 
 
41 
In relation to the previous calculations conducted in the initial phase of the 
methodology, EMB3Rs platform requires not only heat demand but also the mass flow 
data as input for the simulation. On the other hand, the EnergyPRO software solely 
requests heat demand information to conduct simulation.  
Further, it should be highlighted that the initial intention was to utilize the 
EMB3Rs platform for this project. However, due to the unavailability of the platform 
through its development and time limitations, the simulations will ultimately be 
conducted using the EnergyPRO software. As previously mentioned, EnergyPRO stands 
out as the foremost software for modeling and analyzing complex energy systems. It 
provides the capability to conduct feasibility studies and compare various scenarios, 
aligning perfectly with the objectives of this project. 
 
 
3.4 Scenarios 
 
For the selection of the scenarios that are going to be evaluated in this project, 
many possibilities have been taken into account: waste heat, solar, biomass, heat pump… 
But some sources have had to be discarded due to the impossibility of taking heat from 
them. 
According to the consultant, Raadvad is located in a valley. This fact means that 
solar heating would not be an option as it would not take advantage of all the hours of 
sunshine, being already the irradiance in the area quite low in the winter. Moreover, 
underground water is also discarded because ground water is not flowing in the area (it is 
static). Thus, there is risk of freezing in winter, and water will not be renovated as much 
as if the water would flow. Waste heat can neither be an option, as it cannot be extracted 
from the operations of any of the industries located in the surroundings. 
Additionally, energy or heat storage is not going to be taken into account, due to 
space limitations in the area. 
 
==PAGE:42==
        METHODOLOGY AND DATA COLLECTION 
 
42 
Therefore, the next scenarios are the ones that will be evaluated, looking for the 
optimization of the temperatures in the network, and using sources technically available 
in the area. 
• 
Scenario 1: Biomass 
• 
Scenario 2: Centralized heat pump 
• 
Scenario 3: Individual heat pumps 
• 
Scenario 4: Centralized + Boosting heat pump 
The simulations in EnergyPRO software are performed using input data derived 
from heat demand and design parameters, as illustrated in Figure 10. The heat demand 
calculation is conducted in section 3.1 “Heating demand” of this project, while the design 
parameters are determined based on relevant literature specific to each scenario. The four 
scenarios are presented to analyse the results and provide a better approach for future 
development.  
 
Figure 10.- Sketch of scenarios 
==PAGE:43==
       Feasibility study of a local heating system in Raadvad 
 
 
 
43 
Before conducting the simulation of each scenario, several assumptions need to 
be considered, as well as input data and parameters that apply to all four scenarios. 
i. 
Electricity prices 
Firstly, regarding the current persistent upward trend in electricity prices, it is 
important to note that the hourly electricity spot prices selected for this project are based 
on registered data from 2020 in Denmark. 
ii. 
Sinks’ demand 
Further, the heating demand is considered to be dependent on the external 
temperature, varying from the base heat demand obtained. In other words, EnergyPRO 
compares the hourly external temperature throughout a year with a reference temperature 
of 17ºC and determines the specific demand for each sink. The ambient temperature data 
has been obtained from the software, and it is procured from the Climate Forecast System 
Reanalysis (CFSR) in a location close to Raadvad. 
Additionally, it is important to note that there are different heat demand profiles 
for the sinks. For sinks number 1, 1.1, and 1.2, the heat demand remains constant 
throughout the entire day. Sinks 2, 2A and the industries, on the other hand, have a heat 
demand from 9h to 17h. The remaining sinks have a heat demand from 00h to 10h and 
for 16h to 24h.

combined sinks 1, 1.1 and 1.2. It can be seen how demand changes through the year 
dependent on the ambient temperature, being almost 0 in the summer months.

iii. 
Economic data 
Regarding capital expenditure (CAPEX), and the operational expenditures 
(OPEX), data has been obtained from relevant literature from the Danish Energy Agency 
[51]. These expenditures will vary in each scenario and depend on the specific technology 
used. 
Table 6 provides an overview of the expenditures associated with the technologies 
utilized in the simulation. The CAPEX includes the costs of the installation, the 
equipment, and the grid connection (in the case of the heat pumps). 
Table 6.- Expenditures of the technologies used in the scenarios

The project will be in operation for 25 years starting on 01/2024, meaning that 
there will be a linear depreciation of the CAPEX through its lifetime. Moreover, taxes are 
also taken into account in the analysis, and a tax rate of 22% is considered in the 
calculations. 
The price of heat, which serves as the project’s revenues, has been determined 
with a value of 35 €/MWh of heat produced (considering distribution losses) in the initial 
year. This price, as well as the OPEX, are projected to increase annually with inflation, 
which has been set at a rate of 2% based on [52]. Additionally, a discount rate of 4% is 
used, which is the official socio-economic discount rate for projects in Denmark [53]. 
Moreover, three key parameters will be calculated in each scenario to assess their 
feasibility. These are the Net Present Value (NPV), the Internal Rate of Return (IRR) and 
the Pay-back time (PBT). 
- 
The NPV shows how much a project worths in the present. A positive NPV 
indicates that the undertaking of the project is favourable, while a negative one 
suggests to not invest in the project. 
 
==PAGE:45==
       Feasibility study of a local heating system in Raadvad 
 
 
 
45 
- 
The IRR is the discount rate that makes the NPV to become 0. For being 
feasible, a project’s IRR must be higher than the discount rate. 
- 
The Pay-back time indicates the number of years required to recover the initial 
investment. The shorter the PBT, the more attractive the project. 
iv. 
Heat distribution losses 
In accordance with section 2.6 “Heat losses”, losses play a crucial role in these 
systems. Although it has been mentioned that these losses are caused by different factors 
like the length, the diameter of the pipe and the insulation, to assess their effect in the 
different scenarios, a simplified assumption is made. 
In case of considering the heat losses in the distribution system, due to software’s 
limitations, they are going to be accounted in terms of energy demand from the sinks. 
This is, by oversizing the final demand of heat. Thus, parameters such as pipes distances 
or insulation levels will not be considered to account the losses, since it has not been 
possible to obtain specific and optimized information for the project due to the 
impossibility of using EMB3Rs platform.  
This means that in the economic evaluation costs of the pipes are not considered 
as well. 
v. 
Backup boiler 
As explained in section 3.2 “Base case. CO2 emissions”, there are two natural 
gas boilers that will serve as backup system. This element will be common for all the 
scenarios, with an output of 0.9 MW. 
Regarding the economic data, only the fixed OPEX will be considered, as it is 
already installed and will not be actively producing heat. For the natural gas price used in 
the project, the value stated in [54] is taken into account. The price is given as 0.94 €/m3 
and is based on data up to 2020, considering the expectation that actual values will 
decrease. In addition, the heat value of the natural gas is 11.000 kWh/m3. Table 7 shows 
the operational expenditures for the natural gas boilers. 
Table 7.- Expenditures of natural gas boiler 
 
Fixed OPEX 
Variable OPEX

3.4.1 Scenario 1: Biomass 
In line with the main objective of generating heat in a more environmentally 
friendly way, scenario 1 focuses on using biomass as a source to provide energy to 
Raadvad’s residents, as Figure 12 shows. 
 
In this scenario, a biomass boiler that operates with wood chips, wood pellets or 
agricultural waste is utilized to generate heat. As mentioned in the literature, biomass is 
considered a renewable source, as it can be reused in a cycle, thus avoiding dependence 
on fossil fuels and reducing greenhouse gas emissions. 
Figure 13 illustrates the modelled scheme of this particular scenario within the 
EnergyPRO software. For the biomass boiler, only its size is demanded. Therefore, the 
heat output of the biomass boiler is going to be sized according to the heat demand. 
Moreover, the heating value of the biomass is 9.3 GJ/ton or 2.8 GJ/m3 according 
to [55], and its price is stated as 9.4 €/ton, as indicated in [56]. These values are key in 
order to perform a realistic simulation of the scenario analyzed. 
Figure 12.- Scenario 1 sketch 
==PAGE:47==
       Feasibility study of a local heating system in Raadvad 
 
 
 
47 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Distribution losses must also be considered in this scenario as an important factor 
in the design of the DH network. These losses are primarily attributed to the distance 
between the boiler and each sink, and requires oversizing the design to account for the 
energy that will be lost during transmission, as stated in the literature review. Therefore, 
an oversizing factor of 20% is applied to the design of the heating demands in the 
software, so that the right amount of heat is produced and delivered to meet the heating 
demand in each building. On the other hand, losses in a secondary loop inside the 
buildings are assumed to be negligible, as well as localized losses is other elements of the 
network. 
 
 
 
Figure 13.- Scenario 1 in EnergyPRO 
==PAGE:48==
        METHODOLOGY AND DATA COLLECTION 
 
48 
3.4.2 Scenario 2: Centralized heat pump 
Scenario 2 is focused on the design of centralized water-to-water heat pump that 
utilizes the thermal energy of the water from the river as a heat source, thus increasing 
the temperature of the water in the DH network and providing district heating solutions. 
The sketch of this scenario is illustrated in Figure 14. 
 
In this case, and based on the literature, the return and forward temperatures of the 
district heating network will be 35ºC and 70ºC respectively. Additionally, the 
temperatures of the water source are determined to be 4ºC and 3ºC. The scheme of the 
scenario modelled in EnergyPRO can be seen in Figure 15.  
The heat pump is designed to have a COP of 3, and it is dimensioned based on the 
heat output required by the system, thus ensuring that is sized appropriately to meet the 
heating demand and maximizing the efficiency. 
For this scenario, distribution losses are taken into account in the same way as in 
scenario 1, thus heat demand has been oversized 20%. 
 
 
 
Figure 14.- Scenario 2 skecth 
==PAGE:49==
       Feasibility study of a local heating system in Raadvad 
 
 
 
49 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
3.4.3 Scenario 3: Individual heat pumps 
In this section scenario 3 is explained, which involves the design of individual air-
to-water heat pumps for each of the sinks in Raadvad, as the schematic diagram in Figure 
16 shows. 
 
 
Figure 15.- Scenario 2 in EnergyPRO 
Figure 16.- Scenario 3 sketch 
==PAGE:50==
        METHODOLOGY AND DATA COLLECTION 
 
50 
Scenario 3 differs from the previous in terms of that there is no need to oversize the 
heat production to take into account the distribution losses, as there is only a small 
network inside each building, where losses due to transportation are neglected. 
However, it is important to consider that installing the heat pump in close proximity 
to the house might result in additional noise. 
Moreover, regarding the parameters that define the heat pumps and the 
temperatures used, they are similar as those in scenario 2, but with a slight change in the 
supply and return temperatures on the source side. Since the source in this case is the air, 
these temperatures are set at 8ºC (annual mean ambient temperature in the area) and 4ºC 
respectively. 
 
3.4.4 Scenario 4: Centralized + boosting heat pump 
Finally, in this section scenario 4 is explained. This one is a combination between 
scenarios 2 and 3, where there is a centralized heat pump in a low temperature network, 
along with boosting heat pumps to achieve the optimal temperature for DHW 
consumption. The schematic representation of scenario 4 can be observed in Figure 17. 
 
To model the scenario in EnergyPRO, two different simulations are done: one 
which only involves the centralized heat pump, and the other with only the boosting heat 
pumps, due to limitations of the software. 
According to the literature in section 2.5 “Temperatures”, this scenario represents 
the current 4th or 5th generation of district heating. These systems use low temperatures in 
the distribution network to reduce losses and achieve higher efficiencies. 
Figure 17.- Scenario 4 sketch 
==PAGE:51==
       Feasibility study of a local heating system in Raadvad 
 
 
 
51 
For this scenario, the centralized heat pump and the distribution network will 
operate with a return and supply temperatures of 35ºC and 70ºC respectively. The 
temperatures from the source (water from the river) remain unchanged. The 
implementation of a lower temperature network, results in the fact that distribution losses 
are reduced from 20% of the total heat demand in scenario 2 to 12%, as stated in section 
2.6 “Heat losses”. As a results, the heat pump in this scenario will produce 2,388.7 
MWh/year. 
The centralized heat pump, as done before, is sized according to the requested heat 
consumption, and it has a COP of 3. 
On the other part, the boosting heat pumps serve to rise the temperature of the 
network above 60ºC, to prevent the risk of legionella. There are six different heat pumps 
placed to serve grouped sinks, and act as a connection between the distribution network 
and the secondary loop. Therefore, losses are not considered in this second stage and the 
boosting HP are sized specifically to be able to meet the final heat demand, without taking 
into account any additional losses. The boosting heat pumps have also a COP of 3. 
Thus, from the source side, the forward temperature is 40ºC and the return 
temperature 20ºC, while the sink’s side temperatures are 70ºC and 40ºC respectively.

        METHODOLOGY AND DATA COLLECTION

4. RESULTS AND DISCUSSIONS 
In this section, the results and data obtained is presented, together with supporting 
tables and figures. The same procedure as in the previous section is followed, showing 
firstly the heating demand results, and then the data obtained with the simulations 
performed in the software. 
 
4.1 Heat demand results 
 
The total heat demand in the area amounts for 2,102,566 kWh/year, based on the 
calculations explained in 3.13.1 “Heating demand”. This substantial value confirms that 
carrying out a feasibility study of different solutions is essential in the selected town. 
It is remarkable to compare these results with the data presented in the consultant's 
report, as it offers valuable information about the realization of the project. The following 
table presents a comparison between the project's results and the corresponding 
information documented in the report. 
Table 8.- Comparison of the heating demand 
[kWh/year] 
Calculated 
Report 
Housing association 
966,191 
693,000 
Raadvad industries 
815,743 
352,000 
 
The table shows that the calculated results are generally higher than those from 
the report, being around 28% over the ones obtained by the consultant in the case of the 
housing association, but with an even higher difference in the case of the industries, where 
the calculated heat demand is more than twice the demand stated in [43]. The comparison 
with all the sinks can be seen in B “Comparison”. 
 
 
==PAGE:54==
        RESULTS AND DISCUSSIONS 
 
54 
This variation in the heating demand can be attributed to several factors: 
- 
As explained previously, the data from each building was obtained from the 
BBR, but these data might not be updated for each building. In cases where 
renovations or changes have occurred in a building, the heating demand could 
have slightly changed. 
- 
The assumptions made for certain sinks, where they were assumed to be 
similar to the surrounding ones, is also a reason why the values can vary. As 
stated in previous sections, this was necessary due to the absence of data for 
those specific sinks in the BBR. 
- 
If the current use of the building differs from its potential use, this could affect 
also the heating demand, causing deviations in both calculations. Changes in 
the building's purpose can impact the heating demand and result in 
discrepancies between the calculated values and the actual heating 
requirements. This is the case of sink 19, where the report indicates a annual 
consumption of 26400 kWh, while the calculations made resulted in 4147 
kWh per year. 
- 
On sink 11 there is a greater difference in demand that in the rest of the 
buildings. While the report indicates a demand of 17000 kWh, the calculations 
made gave a total of 45901 kWh, almost 3 times more. The reason of this 
deviation is that in the report it is only considered 100 m2 of heated area, while 
the calculations made consider the total are of the buildings, thus being this 
value valid in case of future renovations. 
The variations in heating demand observed in Raadvad's buildings indicate that 
multiple factors influence it. Thus, the following scenarios outlined in this project are 
based on the previously calculated heating demand data (see section 3.1 “Heating 
demand”). 
 
 
 
==PAGE:55==
       Feasibility study of a local heating system in Raadvad 
 
 
 
55 
4.2 Scenario 0. Base case (CO2 emissions) 
 
In this initial scenario, the current CO2 emissions in Raddvad are presented. 
According to section 3.2 “Base case. CO2 emissions”, for all sinks for which data are 
available and which are accounted for in this scenario, the yearly emissions of CO2 
amount for 330 tons. This highlights the number of emissions being released into the 
environment, even though Raadvad is a small area compared to the large cities. Moreover, 
it emphasizes the significance of reducing CO2 emissions even in smaller communities. 
However, if the electricity is considered to come from a renewable source, and the 
emissions caused by wood pellets are neglected due to their cycling process, the overall 
emissions are significantly reduced. In this case, all the emissions will come from natural 
gas boilers, accounting for 252 tons of CO2 every year. This shows the importance of 
utilizing renewable energy sources and minimizing dependence on fossil fuels to achieve 
significant reductions in CO2 emissions. 
The detailed calculations for the emissions in the base case can be found in Annex 
C “Emissions”. 
 
 
4.3 Scenario 1: Biomass plant 
 
The biomass boiler in scenario 1 has been sized based on the maximum heating 
consumption, as indicated in section 3.4.1 “Scenario 1: Biomass”. This peak demand of 
heat amounts 1.2123 MW and occurs in January. Therefore, the boiler has been designed 
with an input power of 1.4 MW, and a heat output of 1.3 MW, as it can reach high 
efficiencies of over 90% according to the literature. 
In Annex D “Heat consumption” (Figure Annex 1) it can be seen the graph that 
shows the variation in heat consumption during a year. Additionally, it also illustrates the 
seasonal variation of heat consumption, where winter months show higher heat 
consumption compared to summer months. 
==PAGE:56==
        RESULTS AND DISCUSSIONS 
 
56 
As expected, from the report “Energy conversion” attached in Annex E “Scenario 
1”(Figure Annex 5 and Figure Annex 6) it can be seen that the biomass boiler fulfills all 
the heat requirements in the scenario, while the natural gas boiler serves as a backup 
system in case of any issue with the biomass boiler. 
Therefore, the biomass boiler operates for a total of 2021.6 full load hours, 
resulting in a utilization factor of 23.01% and a total efficiency of 92.86%. 
In terms of emissions, compared to the base case scenario 1 is considered to not 
have any direct emissions. Although the CO2 emissions amount for a total of more than 
1000 tons of CO2 per year, as biomass can be reused in a cycle, these emissions would 
not be as noxious as in other cases. 
Lastly, Table 9 shows the key investment figures evaluated (NPV, IRR and PBT) 
for the biomass boiler scenario. 
Table 9.- NPV, IRR and Pay-back time of scenario 1 
NPV 
110,622 € 
IRR 
5.20% 
PBT 
21.6 years 
 
The economic result show that the scenario 1 is feasible. This is because NPV>0, 
and then the IRR is higher than the discount rate, which is 4% for public projects 
according to literature. Moreover, the PBT of 21.6 years indicates that the project, 
although late, will start to generate profits before the end of its life. The detailed 
calculations for the economic part are attached in Annex E “Scenario 1”.  
 
 
4.4 Scenario 2: Centralized heat pump 
 
As stated in section 3.4.2 “Scenario 2: Centralized heat pump”, the the sizing of 
the heat pump is determined based on the heating demand. The graph that illustrates the 
==PAGE:57==
       Feasibility study of a local heating system in Raadvad 
 
 
 
57 
variation of heat consumption throughout the year is attached in Annex D “Heat 
consumption”(Figure Annex 2), from which valuable data can be derived. 
For instance, the analysis of the graph indicates that the peak heat consumption 
occurs in December/January, reaching a maximum value of 1.1965MW.  
This peak value is the one that it is used to size the heat pump. Therefore, as the 
electrical consumption is a third of the heat production (COP is equal to 3), the size of the 
heat pump will be 0.4 MWe, thus having an efficiency of 44,9%, and ensuring that these 
dimensions are perfectly adjusted to meet the demand. 
Moreover, from the report named “Energy conversion” (see Annex F “Scenario 
2”) it can be seen that, as expected, the use of the heat pump is the complete year, totalling 
8760 hours. In contrast, the natural gas boiler does not contribute to produce heat. 
 From all the hours the heat pump is working, there are 2190 full-load hours, where 
the heat pump is working at its maximum capacity, thus being the utilization factor a 
24.93%. The more full-load hours, the more adjusted is the size of the heat pump to the 
heating demand. However, working at full capacity for too long may reduce the lifetime 
of the heat pump. 
In terms of environmental impact, scenario 3 has no direct emissions as the only 
input is electricity. Nevertheless, using water from the river may have an environmental 
impact due to the cooling effect at the discharge point. The interview performed with the 
consultancy revealed that the cooling is expected to only be of 1ºC (from 4ºC to 3ºC), 
thus not being harmful for the organisms in the area. 
Finally, the economic data from this scenario is presented. As done in the previous 
one, the key parameters that are going to be used to evaluate this option are the NPV the 
IRR and the PBT of the project. Detailed reports and calculations for assessing the 
feasibility of scenario 2 can be found in F ”Scenario 2”.  
These results are presented in the following table. 
Table 10.- NPV, IRR and Pay-back time of scenario 2

Based on the calculated parameters, scenario 2 demonstrates economic feasibility, 
The positive NPV and the IRR exceeding the discount rate of 4% indicate that the project 
is financially viable. Additionally, the PBT of 14.36 years indicates that the investment 
will be recovered in 2038, after which the project is expected to generate profits. 
 
 
4.5 Scenario 3: Individual heat pumps 
 
In scenario 3, the dimensioning of the air-to-water heat follows the same 
procedure as in the previous one: each heat pump is sized according to the heat output 
required by the sink it is linked to, thus adjusting perfectly to meet the individual heat 
demand of each building. The capacities of the heat pumps range from 1,2 kW to 480 kW 
of heat output, and are shown together with other relevant data in the report titled 
“Catalogue of technical assumptions” (see annex).  
Furthermore, the combined heat consumption amount for 0.9649 MW on its peak, 
and the heating demand has a similar seasonal variation throughout the year as observed 
the previous scenarios, as it is seen in Annex D “Heat consumption”(Figure Annex 3). In 
addition, the mean full load hours and utilization factor of all the heat pumps in this model 
are 3167.37 hours and 36.1% respectively. 
Regarding the environmental data, scenario 3, similar to scenario 2, does not 
release direct emissions as the electricity input is assumed to come from renewable 
energy. 
In addition, the impact of the heat pumps in the environment can be considered 
negligible. Since they use the ambient air as a heat source, the slight temperature change 
from 8ºC to 4ºC of a small amount of air does not have a significant effect on the 
environment. 
Table 11 shows the economic parameter for scenario 3, thus being the complete 
calculations in Annex G “Scenario 3”.

The results show that this scenario is not economically feasible as the NPV is 
negative, thus the recommendation is not to invest in this option. Additionally, the Pay-
back period is larger than the lifetime of the project, thus there will not be any profits 
through its operation. 
It is worth to notice that the yearly depreciations exceed the EBITDA (Earnings 
Before Interest Taxes Depreciation and Amortization), resulting in no taxes being paid 
for this scenario. Nevertheless, the high initial investment still makes unprofitable to 
invest in individual heat pumps as a solution, as the overall costs outweigh the potential 
benefits in this scenario. 
 
 
4.6 Scenario 4: Centralized + individual heat pumps 
 
Regarding the centralized water-to-water heat pump in scenario 4, it has been 
designed with an output of 1.2 MW of heat, to meet the peak consumption of 1.1019MW. 
Thus, its electricity input is 0.4 MW as the COP is 3, and the efficiency of this heat pump 
is 26.1%. Moreover, the annual electricity consumption is 796.2 MWh, and the electric 
heat pump operates for 1990.53 full load hours, which results in a utilization factor of 
22.66%. Detailed results and the heat consumption variation are shown in Annex D “Heat 
consumption”(Figure Annex 4). 
On the other side, the boosting heat pumps are responsible for meeting a combined 
heat demand of 0.97MW, and its size varies depending on the group of sinks they are 
connected to, ranging from 28kW, to 546kW of heat output in the pump that boost the 
industries’ system, and ensuring the required heat output is provided. Additionally, the 
mean heat load hours of all the boosting heat pumps are 2978.82 hours, resulting in a

33.91% of utilization factor. Data involving production of the boosting heat pumps, and 
its dimension can be seen in the annex. 
The CO2 Emissions, as with the other scenarios that involve the use of heat pumps, 
are neglected as considering the electricity is sourced from renewable energy. 
In the economic data for scenario 4, the operational expenditures as well as the 
CAPEX from both the centralized heat pump and the boosting heat pumps are considered. 
However, the revenues are only dependent on what is the heat output of the centralized 
heat pump.

As seen, the analysis indicates that it is not advisable to invest in this option due 
to the high CAPEX and OPEX involved. Despite the potential of low temperature 
networks in district heating as seen in literature, the assumptions made for dimensioning 
the distribution losses have significantly impacted the results. This yields that are less 
revenues while the OPEX are much higher compared to other options. 
 
 
4.7 Comparison of scenarios 
 
This section provides a comparison of the four scenarios simulated in order to 
determine the most suitable option for this particular project. The comparison takes into 
account both technical and economical results. 
Firstly, it is worth mentioning that all four scenarios contribute positively to 
reducing CO2 emissions compared to the base case, which is in Error! Reference source 
not found. “Scenario 0. Base case (CO2 emissions)”. Any of scenarios could lead to zero 
==PAGE:61==
       Feasibility study of a local heating system in Raadvad 
 
 
 
61 
direct emissions as they eliminate the use of natural gas and oil boilers, aligning with the 
main objective of this thesis. 
Table 13 shows the comparison of the four scenarios regarding parameters such 
as peak demand and full load hours. 
Table 13.- Comparison of scenarios 
 
Scenario 1

Based on the table, various insights can be taken. It can be observed that scenario 
3 has a better performance, as the individual heat pumps are sized more precisely to the 
heat demand of the sink. As a result, the peak demand is significantly lower than in 
scenarios 1, 2 and 4. This leads to more full load hours and consequently a higher 
utilization factor compared to the other scenarios. Although excessively high values of 
the utilization factor might be harmful for the heat pump and can reduce its lifetime, the 
variable demand prevents these values to be reached for extended periods. 
Additionally, the electricity demand is aligned with the mentioned parameters, as 
it is seen in the table above. This factor would be particularly relevant in the case where 
electricity does not come from renewable energy, thus scenario 3 would reduce emissions 
in a greater way than the rest of alternatives. 
Nevertheless, the relevant data for selecting a valid option are the economic 
indicators, since at a minimum the project must avoid financial losses. Thus, Table 14 
shows the comparison of the economic key figures for the four different scenarios. 
 
==PAGE:62==
        RESULTS AND DISCUSSIONS 
 
62 
Table 14.- Comparison of the NPV and IRR for the four scenarios 
 
Scenario 1 
Scenario 2 
Scenario 3 
Scenario 4 
NPV 
110,622 € 
279,501.32 € 
-656,605.78 € 
-799,458 € 
IRR 
5.20% 
8.54% 
-1.36% 
-13.19% 
 
From the table above, it can be clearly seen which options should be discarded 
form consideration for the project, as they have negative NPV and IRR lower than 4%. 
On the one side, scenario 3 has the drawback that the required investment is too high due 
to the number of heat pumps involved, and the fact that air-to water heat pumps are more 
expensive than water-to-water ones, further contributes to the higher costs associated with 
this scenario. Moreover, this scenario does not align with the definition of “district 
heating” provided in section 2.1 “District heating”, therefore is not taken into account as 
a valuable solution. 
On the other side, scenario 4, even though it is a promising option in line with the 
actual trend of lowering network temperatures according to relevant literature, provides 
the poorest economic results among the four scenarios. The reason for these results might 
be attributed to the high OPEX, but also the modelling of the scenario could be improved 
to provide more accurate results. 
 With the assumptions made for the heat losses, higher losses actually result in 
higher revenues, which does not provide an advantage for this scenario However, this 
issue could be solved by using alternative assumptions or considering additional factors 
in the analysis. 
Between scenarios 1 and 2, both options demonstrate their feasibility. As the 
project studied is public, the main objective is not to maximize revenues. However, 
scenario 2 stands as the better alternative, not only financially but also in the utilization 
factor of the technology. This makes scenario 2 the preferred option overall. 
Based on the comparison, it can be concluded that installing a centralized heat 
pump that uses water from the river as a heat source is the best option for providing DH 
to the village of Raadvad. This system has a heat pump with a capacity of 1.2 MW of heat 
and operates with temperature networks of 35/70ºC, making it the most suitable scenario.
